Eric S. said...
@Rabbter: I’m not sure what school of thought Nestorian follows with YEC, but the ones I’ve encountered typically answer that question with the position that the heavens and the earth, along with the first life forms were created fully mature, and what appears to be the result of older processes is in fact an illusion cast by the establishment of a mature creation. The light from stars appears to bear evidence of a process of stellar creation for the same reason that a newly created tree or animal would already have growth rings on their bones or trunks despite never having been born/sprouted. Even though he created the cosmos at once, he created it to follow the patterns and forms of the rules he laid down for it, so that it might appear very old except for what had appeared in the scriptures. The slightly more eccentric theory I grew up with was called the Gap Theory and posited that the evidences of an old earth and cosmos were remnants from a prior creation which was destroyed, and that the beginning of Genesis is better read as “the earth became without form and void,” but that particular view was a fringe view even within creation research. The mature creation theory is part of the reason creationists insist on a distinction between “experimental” and “historical” science, since what appear to be long unfolding processes according to the present laws of nature may well be illusions cast from creation having been initiated in order to follow certain natural patterns. It does a good job of preventing the philosophy from becoming easily falsified, but it also seems to me like it could just as easily be used to support the theory that the earth was created last week, and our memories of life before then are implanted… It turns into one of those philosophies like the brain in a vat theory that is fun to play with but difficult to find a practical use for unless you have a strong investment in it being true. And, since there’s nothing in Christian theology that explicitly requires young earth creationism being true, with evolution being officially accepted by most mainline Christian denominations… and there’s only pressure to believe in it if you’re invested in one very particular form of literalist interpretation of scripture (there are biblical literalist ways to argue for theistic evolution and old earth creationism)… it is very difficult to convince anyone who disagrees… and even people who are invested are going to have to devote a lot of time and energy to defending their stance against every new piece of data that comes in independently without stepping back to look at the big picture (at least that was my experience as a YEC when I was younger).

@JMG: That’s what all you false prophets say. You promise us that the gods you’re heralding in are cute and little, and the next thing we know we’re lying prostrate before them, sacrificing children and chanting in the ancient tongue while they tear our cities to the ground ;-).

John Roth said...
@Eric S.

Quite true. When your answer to every embarrassing question is “God did it that way,” then you’ve lost all hope of credibility other than to your co-religionists.

What probably surprises a lot of people in the anti-evolution camp is that most evolutionary biologists have no objection in principle to having God, gods, space aliens or whatever having some influence on the process. They just want evidence other than “you can’t explain this” and “it’s irreducibly complex.” To date, that evidence has not been forthcoming.

Granted, it’s a pretty high bar to cross. Proving that God had its thumb on the process is tantamount to proving that God exists. Finding a space alien’s tentacle-prints on the process is tantamount to not only proving that they exist, but they’ve been hanging around for a while.

Steve in Colorado said...
I find it interesting (perhaps amusing) that so much effort is being put into discussions on the "scientific method". It seems that over the course of history whatever structures/science/myths humans put together to describe their world were chosen because they worked at the time. It did not matter whether they are "correct" when viewed from the modern knowledge base (anymore than whether our science will be right or wrong when viewed from some future understanding of the universe), they worked. They were useful tools at the time. If they weren't useful they were quickly discarded.

Worked in this case means different things. There were certainly cultures who could predict the movements of planets and predict eclipses without a sun-centered view of the universe. Worked also means in the social and political sense in preserving the status quo ("God is always right, the church and king are his spokespeople on earth, obey them").

As JMG has said, we have elevated the scientific method to the status of a god and it and its promise of progress have become our modern religion. But really they serve no other purpose than the myths and religious hierarchies of the past; they explain a bit of the world and serve to knit together the existing order of the day. Do they have their flaws, sure; and when they stop doing what they really are supposed to do, we have a revolution of one sort or another; as a new paradigm replaces the old.

That is the big story we are watching unfold. Whether in this upcoming election, climate change, social unrest they are all just part of the story. So pull up a chair, pass the popcorn and have some of your favorite beverage; may as well enjoy the show...

7/19/16, 5:35 PM

Unknown said...
(Deborah Bender)

One more observation on changing social attitudes toward vaccines. These days we are constantly pestered to donate money for research to cure various medical problems. I remember only one campaign of this kind in my childhood: the March of Dimes. Its goal was to find a cure for polio. It was highly publicized and people went door to door collecting donations. The purchasing power of a dime was equivalent to more than a dollar today, and dimes had Franklin Roosevelt's head on them.

Instead of a cure for polio, they found a means of preventing it, which was a great victory for public health. I believe Dr. Jonas Salk donated his patent rights, so won fame but not fortune. Big Pharma wasn't so big in those days, though the time of cranking out poorly tested new drugs to generate profits wasn't far off. The turning point for people's trust in medical advances was probably when they found out about the effects of thalidomide.

It didn't escape my notice that when the March of Dimes achieved its goal, it did not declare victory and close up shop. Instead it picked a different medical problem that can never be completely fixed (birth defects) and went right on collecting money.

rabtter said...
Hi Again Nestorian,

Your comment here,

"...the results of empirical science across the disciplines is in fact fraught with anomalies, apparent contradictions,"

Are you talking about things like moon dust and paluxy river bed tracks? Be wary of claimed anomolies. About 15 years I took on the task of fact checking a small book two preachers that frequented the area wrote. They relied heavily on writers like Morris, Gish, Thompson, Wysong, the usual suspects. I spent several years locating original sources for the claims. Many trips to a University Geology Library, ordering copies of articles in old out of print journals. I didn't make it all the way to the end (I had had enough), but I fact checked a couple dozen of the claimed anomolies and none of them panned out.

One did stick in my mind though, was an alleged claim by Bert Thompson of wagon tracks at a fossil bed in Texas which the geologist studying the place claimed were made by snakes. The citation for this was an audio tape by Bert Thompson, I purchased the audio tape to check it out, no mention of wagon tracks.

I was a regular visitor to the usenet group talk.origins at the time and had access to some paleontologists. Finally somebody gave me a name of who may have worked the site. I contacted him and concluded he was the right person after some correspondence. The property owner was indeed convinced the two parallel marks were wagon tracks, and was continually presenting him with plain old rocks and asking what kind of fossil it was. I asked about the snakes, he said that he did not think it was snakes at the time but really didn't know what made the marks. A couple of years later, he saw marks on a beach that he called "essentially the same" that were made by the outgoing tide dragging debris.

I confronted the preacher sometime later at a family member's house and asked about the tracks and explained that it wasn't on the citation given. He claimed to be live at that particular presentation and yes Bert Thompson did mention the tracks. I think his claim for that is something I can accept. I speculate that someone pointed out to Dr. Thompson that there were Dinosaur tracks all around, but there were no tracks left by a draught animal pulling the wagon, and thus it was removed from audio tape that was sold to the public.

In the meantime, some skeletons began rattling very loudly in Dr. Thompson's closet and he found it prudent to retire permanently to seclusion. A decade later he's still in seclusion so I can't find out in his own words the reasoning for removing from the audio that was sold.

Now the confrontation with the preacher. I wish I had handled this confrontation differently, some family were mad at me. The preacher, in his defense did say he will accept correction and as proof of that tabled paluxy as something he willingly gave up after visiting the site. He said he had some new stuff to send up to me, but never did. A year or so after that his home congregation kicked him out due to a problem arising from or related to plain old money, anybody I asked was embarrassed and really didn't want to talk about it. He hasn't been back to my area since that day.


There was some drama with that, but most fact checking is dull. The TalkOrigins website and google group are good resources for fact checking, lots of stuff there for Morris. Also check the primary source on which they base the claim.

Also stay up to date with creationist stance on various claims, many of the anomolies that were claimed 20 years ago have now been abandoned.

rabtter said...
Hi again Nestorian,

I wanted to respond to your comment to W. B.

Falsifiability.

Well, yeah, its not difficult to see the difficulty. Consider the two statements,

1. Is X falsifiable?
2. Is X falsifiable with any of the available empirical data we have?

If the answer to 1 is yes it does not strictly mean the answer to 2 is yes.

With regard stretching evolution to fit empirical data consider the nature of the empirical data in question. If paleontologists starting chiseling fossil kangaroos out of the Middle Devonian basins in Norway starting tomorrow, I don't think there's anyway you can stretch current Evolutionary Theory to account for it. The theory itself may not be falsified, but some filled in blanks in its notional space would be. Creation Theory would have no problem with it.

Consider if a new unknown species was discovered that looked like a rabbit, is genetically close to a rabbit, yet it laid eggs from which its young hatched. No problem for Creation Theory, but the chaos it would cause for Evolutionary Theory would be unimaginable.

Okay, consider the punctuated equilibrium you mentioned. Falsifying it wouldn't falsify evolution as its just a filled in blank in its notional space, and filled in blanks in the notional space have been falsified before. Lamarckism was falsified, now we have epigenetics that has a very small overlap with it. Recapitulation was falsified, but embryology remains. Punctuated Equilibrium at some future date will be refined, or replaced with something that overlaps with it.

If you falsify some small filled in blank in Evolutionary Theory, you're just removing a blemish and ultimately end up improving it; essentially giving it a shave and a haircut. If you want to take a real bite out it, go for a major organ like Common Descent.

To go after Common Descent, you really the Nested Hierarchy and the various means it can be recovered. It you get a good handle on it you'll understand why I can make seemingly silly predictions like:

1. A Kangaroo Rat in southwest US genetically is closer to a Killer Whale than it is to
the Marsupial Mouse of Australia
2. A rat is genetically is closer to a giraffe than it is to a possum.

@ed boyle--I have often thought about our bigger-brained ancestors and your comment gave me more to think about. I have many questions that will probably never be answered. Unless human understanding of the nature of consciousness progresses, at least with respect to understanding how the world is experienced by differently-brained current humans and by other intelligent species, we probably will be unable even to imagine most of the possible answers.

For example:
Did newborn Cro-Magnon infants have bigger heads than human babies today? If so, how did the mothers manage to bear them? Was the mortality rate from childbirth higher? If the infants had the same size heads as now, to grow to full size their childhood development must have been different from modern children, perhaps in duration, in personality development, and who knows what else.
Brain tissue is one of the most metabolically expensive kinds of tissue to maintain. What was the Cro-Magnon diet?
If they had 150 ccm more brain than us, but smaller frontal lobes, what were those extra neurons doing? I suppose we can make some wild guesses by comparison with what little we know about the brains of other intelligent mammals. The only guess I have any confidence in is that they probably had a much keener sense of smell. That's what a large part of a dog's brain is devoted to. Maybe Cro-Magnons had an innate sense of direction, better memories. Maybe they were more intuitive or telepathic.

I read a science fiction short story called House of Bones about a band of homo sapiens in a recent ice age when mammoths flourished and Neanderthals were still around. It was a small band and one of their practices was that each of them had a name for each of the rest of them, and all the names were different, so if there were say fifteen people in the group, each of them had fifteen names and each person called you by the name he or she had given you. This seemed plausible to me. If your group's material culture can be mastered by the age of fifteen and basic survival needs don't take all your waking hours to procure, there is plenty of time to develop complicated non-material culture.

Scotlyn said...
Rabbter, Lynn Margulis has presented symbiogenesis as the theory that overlaps "punctuated equilibrium". She spent the years following her unusual science education (it involved reading the original works of Newton, Darwin and others, no textbooks, and designing and carrying out experiments that weren't pre-prepared), observing organisms, esp bacteria, archebacteria & protists to develop her theory that *novelty* in biology - new tissues, new organs, new ways of organising biological exploitation of the environment, arose from symbiogenic events - different organisms doing something to or with each other to produce something novel. She proposed that this explains novelty in a way that slow accumulation of random mutations simply can't. Her proposal is that a complex cell is a self-perpetuating community comprising different types of bacterial and archebacterial members. She was only able to gain acceptance from her "peers" for the bacterial origins of organelles such as mitichondria and plastids, which retain their own DNA, but until she died she was fleshing out a bigger more comprehensive picture including several other symbiotic events occurring to make up the LECA, the last eukaryotic common ancestor.

Well, I don't want to hijack this thread or this site, so I will keep my rejoinders on YEC brief again.

First, anomalies litter practically all the scientific disciplines insofar as they concern themselves with events in the remote past. A good book tallying up the anomalies that have arisen in the biological sciences in-depth is Duane Gish's "Creation Scientists Confront Their Critics." It's somewhat older, from the early 90s, and so does not take into account the more recent controversies in the Intelligent Design arena. Nevertheless, the anomalies Gish identifies are fundamental, and haves have by no means gone away in the interim.

And the Morris book that I mentioned several times represents a comprehensive attempt to identify the main anomalies in evolutionary thinking to be found across as many scientific disciplines as possible. The chapters on thermodynamics and the origin of life are particularly well done, in my opinion.

There are tons of other books and articles as well, dealing in depth with all of the various scientific disciplines, but this is not the time or place to develop a comprehensive annotated biography. So I encourage any one who has an interest in the matter to read the book for themselves, and try to identify its alleged scientific inadequacies with as much specificity as possible.

7/20/16, 8:28 AM

Eric S. said...

Some more thoughts here as we start to crunch up near the end of the week, since we're starting to get into my territory:

Re: Punctuated Equilibrium: I remember in the previous debate, punctuated equilibrium was presented as an example of evolutionary biology begging the question. Punctuated equilibrium is basically just the law of succession in ecology applied to the fossil record. The basic idea is that most of the time, earth’s biosphere acts as a climax community, and it takes special circumstances to open up a proliferation of open niches that allow evolution to take place (either through niche formation, or through a catastrophe). One well documented example is the period of adaptive radiation that took place during the Paleocene epoch, the extinction of the dinosaurs wiped out most of the planet’s terrestrial K-selected species and what was left were mostly pioneer species and r selected generalists. Usually the process of selection is handled through migration, but if there are no k-selected species to settle a niche, either because there are none left, or because the niche has not yet been occupied, the process of evolution has to take over. In the case of the K-T extinction event, then, generalists such as mammals and small feathered dromeosaurs responded to intra-species competition by branching out into non-competing roles to differentiate their niches, which then created behavioral isolation on once unified populations, as well as selective pressure for specialization within each separate population. After about ten million years, with the beginning of the Eocene, all of the modern orders of mammals and birds we’re familiar with were around, and the earth had recovered once more to the point of having a mature biosphere, from there it took another 30 million years or so to reach the climax communities of the early Neogene, from which point earth’s biodiversity remained fairly stable until quaternary extinction event (which started with the last glacial period before humans came onto the scene and is still going on.)

Re Intelligent Design: My main issue there is that Intelligent Design, much like Creationism expands the definition of evolution to include things that are in no way a part of the theory of evolution. Evolutionary biology doesn’t concern itself with the origin of life, it concerns itself with the mechanism by which populations change over time (which is a far less controversial topic). There aren’t a whole lot of gaps in the step by step process of radiation from a common ancestor… rather the gaps into which intelligent design may still have some room to probe is the transition from the first proto-cells to the first living cells. Protocells are pretty easy to create under naturally occurring conditions, they even sometimes have primitive RNA chains inside… The problem is finding the transition from those protocells to fully functioning cells with ribosomes and complex enzymes (there are theories, but last I checked not much in the way of evidence). If there was an intelligent designer, that’s the level at which it would operate, and it certainly fits into the realm of reasonable hypothesis (though, it’s certainly not a scientific hypothesis, since the scientific method isn’t going to be able to detect a non-material cause… it’ll show up as an anomaly.) Even if we filled in that particular gap, there'd still be room for an intelligent designer. But once you’ve got a cell capable of DNA replication, evolution becomes something organisms do through well documented processes. I personally think that most of the arguments I’ve encountered in Intelligent Design (complexity, etc.) are best explained by the concept of emergence (but then, I tend to view gods themselves as emergent properties of a complex cosmos), and I think emergence has profound spiritual implications, but that’s a topic for the other blog.

@Steve in Colorado
My philosophy on truth is basically:
1) no model can ever be fully accurate, because in order to be fully accurate, it would have to account for everything in the universe, which would make it as infinitely complex as the universe itself (it is impossible to "wall off" a section of the universe and describe just that with your model, because everything in the universe is linked in one way or another)
2) since all models are therefore approximations, it is even possible for two models to be contradictory in their implications and yet both be "true" (once I realized this, I began to understand some of the cryptic passages I'd come across from Buddhist sources)
3) practically speaking, models that are more wrong BUT faster and less resource-intensive to put in practice are very often preferable, because as humans we have neither infinite time nor infinite resources. This is especially so in smaller, poorer population centres.
4) one shouldn't worry too much about whether the mechanisms that any one model gives to explain itself are ACTUALLY TRUE (see point "1"). Just worry about whether it explains what you need it to well enough and that it is easy to use. Newton's "invisible gravitational forces" were considered to be the equivalent of New Age kookery in his day - no less strange than seeing the forests as being full of nature spirits. Whatever works for the task at hand is the right thing to use. Many quantum physicists have given up on explanations entirely, "so long as the math seems to work". Likewise, in my daily life, I often notice that A leads to B but am not sure exactly why - nor is it usually necessary to figure it out (although it does make it frustrating when trying to explain my reasons to other people).

Agent Provocateur said...
Nestorian,

I've seen this same line of thought in some of my Christians friends. You can do as you like of course, but I'm a little concerned with this type of scriptural interpretation because I think it is too brittle. Prove one element not to be historical true and the whole edifice crumbles. Efforts to avoid this then leads to some rather desperate reasoning.

Stories in scripture need not all be understood as literal historical truth. One example will suffice. As a Christian you likely believe Jesus is the Son of God, the Word of God, and the Lamb of God. At some point, if you were forced to say what these three descriptions mean, metaphor would sneak in. It would be hard to maintain Jesus was/is literally a lamb, a word, or perhaps even a son. Something can be true (in terms of values) but not historical fact. And then again there are some things in the scriptures of the world that are perhaps neither. Such elements are then at least useful to provoke thought and encourage listening to one's heart.

A given scriptural story may be metaphorical, didactic, historical, historical fiction, folklore, epic, fable, parable, poetry, myth, prose, polemic, analogy, etc. ... perhaps more than one of these at any one time. Allowing non literal scriptural interpretation, often more than one at a time, has a long history in both Jewish and Christian thought. Its only as Christianity drifted westward into less literate (and so less sophisticated) societies that things got dumbed down. Allowing multiple (and not necessarily literal) interpretations allows a much greater depth and resilience to one's faith. It also helps avoid unnecessary conflict with scientific facts as they are commonly understood today.

I'm not trying to change your mind. But if someday you find the physical evidence of a very old earth too hard to ignore, I'm suggesting you need not drop your faith altogther.

John Roth said...
@Unknown

Cro-Magnon is an obsolete name for humans who lived in Europe after the demise of the Neanderthals and about 10,000 years ago. They were anatomically modern humans. The variety of human with bigger brains was Neanderthals. There's a lot of obsolete information out there; ancient DNA research is clarifying the relationships wonderfully.

@heather

Yeah, generally short adjectives and adverbs take the -er and -est suffixes, longer ones take more and most, or less and least. Of course there are exceptions. This is English we’re talking about.

@Scotlyn

Time is long. Most people don’t get how long; there’s lots of room in there for things we have absolutely no inkling of to have happened as support for what we see now.

The mitochondria story has never made sense to me, for one simple reason. Most of the genes that are needed to support mitochondria are in the cell nucleus; only some of those seem to be clearly bacterial in origin. Where did the rest come from? One answer has turned up recently: an archaea which has a primitive version of organelles and the supporting mechanisms. That makes more sense - the more of the supporting structure that’s there already, the less strange the story that says an archaeon swallowed a bacterium and then they settled their differences.

@onething

Maybe this bit of my personal history will help clarify things. Some time ago I ran across a book titled “The Aquatic Ape,” where the author explained a lot of the differences between humans and great apes by analogy with the whales and dolphins, and proposed that our ancestors had spent a period of time in the sea so those adaptations could occur. With what I knew then, she was quite convincing.

It took me learning a great deal about paleoanthropology and the actual evidence, both fossil and anatomical, to see the gaping holes in her logic. This didn’t happen by reading a book or a blog post or two. It took a couple of years of poking at this and that until I finally came to a conclusion.

When someone says: “you don’t understand the science,” the subtext is “you don’t seem willing to learn, and I have no interest in arguing with you.” People can always find “anomalies” in anything. If you want, you can generate enough “anomalies” to tax the patience of a saint. I tend to call this “argument by tsunami,” and all it does is make me want to write off the arguer as someone who’s more interested in winning, fair means or foul, rather than engaging in a joint effort to find the truth.

“Irreducible complexity” is not a hard problem. It’s an example of “petite principo” or assuming the conclusion, known in philosophy and law as “begging the question.” Pointing at something and saying “that’s irreducibly complex,” isn’t convincing without a solid, experimentally verifiable explanation of exactly why it’s irreducibly complex.

@Esn

On models. I recently came across the distinction between “functional” models and “mechanistic” models. The latter use real pieces with known characteristics and known interactions. Are they simplifications? Of course. Functional models, on the other hand, simply invent parts that act the way the modeler thinks they ought to act.

This may make a bit more sense.

Emmanuel Goldstein said...
During the time of the Terror after the French Revolution, a priest, a lawyer, and an engineer were taken to the guillotine for execution. The priest went first, but when they pulled the rope, the blade stayed aloft.
"Thanks be to God!" cried the priest, "God has preserved my life!"
The executioner let him go.
The lawyer was next. Once again, the blade did not budge.
"You have to set ME free too!" said the lawyer. "If you try again, it's double jeopardy and therefore illegal."
Grumbling, they set the lawyer free too.
The engineer was last. Once again, they pulled the rope and the blade did not move.
As they were untying the engineer and preparing to set him free, the engineer looked up at the Guillotine and said,
"There's a little piece of wood stuck up there. Hey, I think I can fix that..."

7/20/16, 6:12 PM

Myriad said...
Since I'm waiting for tonight's post, and the conversation about origin theories has remained robust for longer than I expected. I'll explain my own view of intelligent design, even though the timing is such that few will likely read it. Perhaps just as well, because this view seems to please neither scientists nor skeptics nor Creationists. I think the results of evolution look like intelligent designs because biological evolution exhibits, or embodies, a form of intelligence.

There are systems in the world that can both generate and preserve persistent complex patterns, a type of behavior only recently recognized as possible. These systems include the functioning of brains, certain iterative processes arising in pure mathematics, and the behavior of computing machines when programmed in certain ways. The other, and foremost in some ways, example is biological evolution.

Even after these processes were known to exist, their creative power was not recognized at first. For instance, well after the process of evolution was roughly understood, some philosophers still claimed that there must be some occult force ("élan vital") separate from evolution itself driving evolution toward more complex forms. Analogously, a great majority of people still believe that there must be some supernatural phenomenon separate from the human brain that makes us conscious.

What I find particularly interesting is that all of those systems also have deep similarities in how they work internally. They are all composed of a large number of interconnected units with their own states, and in all cases the units integrate multiple inputs to change their states in a manner exhibiting sharp changes at a threshold. The logic gates in a computer resolve two or more inputs into a single 1 or 0 output. Brains consist of neurons interconnected via axons and dendrites, each with their own states, integrating the inputs of excitatory and inhibitory synapses against a threshold to fire or not fire moment by moment. (In number theory, the threshold behavior is a bit more abstract, but it's always implicitly present in operations that generate complex patterns. For example, various operations such as addition can combine multiple integers into a single result, but that result is either prime or not, an all-or-nothing threshold of sorts.)

There's a sharp all-or-nothing threshold at the heart of evolution as well. An organism's genome, after very complex interaction with the environment and other organisms, either contributes directly to the genomes of offspring or it does not. That process, going on simultaneously among trillions of genomes (each of them vastly complex themselves) throughout the earth's biosphere, has unimaginably vast computational power.

Computational power alone doesn't imply intelligence, although as far as we know it's a prerequisite for intelligence. But when you see computational power put into effect to generate novel forms that effectively solve complex problems (chiefly, in this case, the ever-changing problem of ongoing survival in the environment), why not call it intelligent? Unless, like Turing, you withhold that designation until it talks to you in your own language.

I can't say whether or not that intelligence experiences, understands, wills, plans, or feels, but it's likely that it remembers (the collective genome is a—I keep using this word—vast memory store), and obvious that it creates.

7/20/16, 7:24 PM

zerowastemillennial said...
Oop, came across a very relevant blog post today that cites some relevant quotes: https://jnanayuddha.wordpress.com/2016/07/20/the-depoliticized-ideology-of-tech-work-then-and-now/

As computer programmers, our formative intellectual experience is working with deterministic systems that have been designed by other human beings. These can be very complex, but the complexity is not the kind we find in the natural world. It is ultimately always tractable. Find the right abstractions, and the puzzle box opens before you. The feeling of competence, control and delight in discovering a clever twist that solves a difficult problem is what makes being a computer programmer sometimes enjoyable. But as anyone who’s worked with tech people knows, this intellectual background can also lead to arrogance. People who excel at software design become convinced that they have a unique ability to understand any kind of system at all, from first principles, without prior training, thanks to their superior powers of analysis. Success in the artificially constructed world of software design promotes a dangerous confidence.

About This Archive

This archive contains the posts and comments from John Michael Greer's blog, The Archdruid Report, from May 2006 to June 2017.

Follow Greer's current writing on his new blog, www.ecosophia.net, or on his Dreamwidth account, ecosophia.dreamwidth.org.

The scripts used to produce this archive are hosted on GitHub. A downloadable copy of the archive is available on Google Drive.

Contact the archive creator at squirrel2038 at gmail.com.

About JMG

John Michael Greer is Past Grand Archdruid of the Ancient Order of Druids in America , current head of the Druidical Order of the Golden Dawn, and the author of more than thirty books on a wide range of subjects, including peak oil and the future of industrial society. He lives in Cumberland, MD, an old red brick mill town in the north central Appalachians, with his wife Sara.

Well of Galabes

If you enjoy this blog and can handle discussions of Druidry, magic, and occult philosophy, you might like my other blog, Well of Galabes.
